No Obligation, Pure Grace, Obligatory Faith
Sanusi explains the meaning of 'no obligation' and 'by pure grace', and how faith in messengers becomes obligatory despite the voluntary sending.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while sending messengers is a voluntary favor from God, belief in those messengers is obligatory.

Core Idea
Divine grace is free, but our response to it is required.

This passage clarifies a subtle theological balance: God is not compelled to send messengers, yet He graciously does so. But that does not make belief optional — it becomes a duty once the message arrives. Sanusi unpacks the phrases of the poem to defend this position against various sects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice how the commentator explains each phrase in order.
The passage moves from negation (no obligation) to affirmation (pure grace) to a corrective (faith now obligatory).
2
Understand 'no obligation'
Focus on the first part: why does Sanusi say sending messengers is not obligatory for God? Who claims it is obligatory (Mu'tazila, Philosophers) and who claims it is impossible (Sumaniyya, Brahmins)?
This reframes the debate between obligation and impossibility.
3
Analyze 'by pure grace'
See how the phrase 'by pure grace' refutes both extremes: 'by His beneficence' counters the Philosophers (who saw it as necessary), and 'the pure' counters the Mu'tazila (who saw it as obligatory).
Pure grace means no compulsion or expectation.
4
Grasp the obligation of faith
The third line addresses a potential confusion: if sending is merely possible, why must we believe? The poet corrects this by asserting that faith in the messengers becomes obligatory once the message is known.
The demonstrative 'by this' is key — it refers to the sending and the messengers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَلَا وُجُوْبَ) أي: إذا علمت أن إرسال الرسل من الجائز العقلي في حقه تعالى، فاعلم أنه لا وجوب عليه تعالى خلافاً للمعتزلة والفلاسفة؛ أي: ولا استحالة، خلافاً للسُّمَنية والبراهمة، كما يُعلم ممَّا تقدم، فالتفريع فيه قصور، ولعله لم يعتد بالقول بالاستحالة.
وقوله : (بَلْ بِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ) أي : بل إرسال الرسل إنما هو بإحسانه الخالص، فإضافة (محض) بمعنى الخالص للفضل بمعنى الإحسان من إضافة الصفة للموصوف، فقولنا : (بإحسانه) فيه ردُّ على الفلاسفة، وقولنا : (الخالص) فيه رد على المعتزلة، و (بل) هنا للإضراب الانتقالي.
قوله : (لَكِنْ بذَا إِيْمَانُنَا قَدْ (وَجَبَا لمَّا كان قد يُتوهَّمُ من كون الإرسال من الجائز العقلي أن الإيمان بوقوعه ليس واجباً .. استدرك عليه بقوله : (لكن بذا إيماننا قد وجبا) بألف الإطلاق، والمتبادر من كلام المصنف أن اسم الإشارة عائد على الإرسال، لكن جعله الشارح عائداً على المذكور من الإرسال والمرسلين.
His saying: 'So there is no obligation' means: when you know that sending messengers is among the rationally possible with respect to Him, exalted, then know that there is no obligation upon Him, exalted—contrary to the Mu'tazila and the Philosophers; that is, and no impossibility, contrary to the Sumaniyya and the Brahmins, as is known from what preceded. So the deduction is deficient, and perhaps he did not take the claim of impossibility into account. And His saying: 'but by pure grace' means: rather, sending messengers is only by His pure beneficence. So the addition of 'pure' means the pure for 'grace' meaning beneficence, as an attribute of the described. So our saying 'by His beneficence' is a refutation of the Philosophers, and our saying 'the pure' is a refutation of the Mu'tazila, and 'but' here is for digression. His saying: 'But by this our faith has become obligatory'—since it might be imagined from the fact that sending is rationally possible that faith in its occurrence is not obligatory, he corrected this with his saying: 'But by this our faith has become obligatory' with the alif of absolute (meter). The apparent meaning from the author's speech is that the demonstrative pronoun refers to the sending, but the commentator made it refer to what was mentioned, i.e., the sending and the messengers.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the key phrases of verse 57 and 58, explaining the doctrinal implications. First, 'so there is no obligation' clarifies that sending messengers is not obligatory for God, refuting the Mu'tazila and Philosophers who claimed obligation. It also implicitly denies impossibility, refuting the Sumaniyya and Brahmins. The author notes the deduction is somewhat deficient, perhaps because the author of the poem (Ibn al-Mu'taz? Actually the commentator) did not consider the impossibility claim as a serious challenge. Second, 'but by pure grace' stresses that sending is purely an act of divine beneficence—the phrase 'by His beneficence' refutes the Philosophers who saw it as necessary, and 'the pure' refutes the Mu'tazila who saw it as obligatory (since obligation would diminish pure grace). The particle 'but' indicates a transition from the negative (no obligation) to the positive (it is a favor). Third, the verse 'But by this our faith has become obligatory' addresses a potential misunderstanding: if sending is only a rational possibility, one might think that believing in its occurrence is not required. The poet corrects this by asserting that faith in the messengers is indeed obligatory. The commentator notes that the demonstrative 'this' most naturally refers to the sending itself, but a different commentator (ash-Sharh? Possibly al-Sharqawi?) interpreted it as referring to the collection of sending and messengers. This highlights that faith in the messengers is obligatory, even though the act of sending is a voluntary favor from God.

Key Terms
فلا وجوب
بمحض الفضل
الإحسان الخالص
الإيمان
الاستدراك
اسم الإشارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If sending messengers is a mere possibility, believing in them is also optional.
Correction: The passage clarifies that faith in messengers becomes obligatory once the message is delivered, even though the sending itself is a voluntary grace from God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the function of the phrase 'but by pure grace' (بل بمحض الفضل)?
A) It emphasizes that sending messengers is a favor, refuting the Philosophers and Mu'tazila.
B) It shows that sending messengers is obligatory.
C) It denies the possibility of sending messengers.
D) It encourages Muslims to abandon reason.

Key Takeaway
Sending messengers is a pure grace from God, not an obligation, yet once sent, belief in them becomes a duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just been reminded that God does not owe you guidance, yet He sent it anyway. Is your faith today a response to that free gift, or have you been treating it as something you deserved? What would change if you truly lived with the awareness that every prophet, every revelation, is an undeserved grace from God?
?
The passage says faith in the messengers is obligatory, not optional. But in your daily life, do you act as if obeying the Prophet (peace be upon him) is optional? Where specifically have you been lax in following his commands? What step will you take today to treat his words as binding upon you?
?
Consider those who denied prophethood entirely (the Brahmins). While you may not be one of them, do you sometimes live as if the messengers have no real authority—choosing your own desires over their teachings? How can you deepen your conviction in the necessity of following the prophets, starting with this lesson?

The Number of Prophets is Not Specified
Commentator explains why it is better not to enumerate all prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic scholars advise against specifying a fixed number for all prophets and the distinction between detailed and summary belief.

Core Idea
It is better not to count all prophets; we believe in them in summary.

In this commentary, the author explains a line from the poem that says 'all the messengers'. He clarifies that the preferred position is not to limit the prophets to a specific number, because the exact count is unknown. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this stance and the important distinction between prophets we must know individually and those we believe in collectively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the commentator's use of 'الأولى عدم حصرهم في عدد' (the better view is not to limit them to a number).
This is the central teaching.
2
Understand the distinction between detailed and summary belief
The commentator references an earlier explanation about prophets who must be believed in detail (like those named in the Quran) and those believed in summary (the rest). Reflect on why this distinction matters.
It helps avoid unnecessary speculation.
3
Consider the advantage of not specifying a number
The phrase 'جميع الرسل' in the poem implies that the exact number is unknown. Why would it be problematic to claim a fixed number? Think about the risk of including false prophets or excluding true ones.
This guards against false claims.
4
Connect to broader Islamic theology
Recognize that this teaching reflects a methodological principle: in matters of faith, we stick to what is revealed and avoid speculation. How does this principle apply to other theological topics?
It shows the importance of adhering to revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : فيه زيادة للبيان كما هو المطلوب في عقائد الإيمان. وقد سبق أوَّلَ الكتاب بيانُ من يجب الإيمانُ بهم تفصيلاً، ومن يجب الإيمان بهم إجمالاً (۱) ، والأولى عدم حصرهم في عدد كما يشعر به قول المصنف : (جميع الرسل)، فإنه يؤذن بعدم معرفة عددهم .
I say: In it there is an increase in clarification as is required in the creeds of faith. And it has preceded at the beginning of the book an explanation of those who must be believed in detail, and those who must be believed in summary (1), and the better [view] is not to limit them to a number as is suggested by the author's saying: (all the messengers), for it indicates the lack of knowledge of their number.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the poem that uses the phrase 'all the messengers'. The commentator explains that the author has already clarified at the beginning of the book which prophets must be believed in individually (in detail) and which collectively (in summary). The commentator then states that the better position is not to specify a fixed number for the prophets, because the phrase 'all the messengers' implies that their exact count is unknown. This guards against the common misunderstanding that one must know the exact number of prophets for correct belief.

Prophetic Attributes: Scriptural Basis and Miracle Evidence
Explains the scriptural basis for prophetic attributes and the differing views on miracle evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of prophetic attributes is based on scripture (samʿī) and how scholars differ on the evidentiary nature of miracles.

Core Idea
Prophetic attributes are necessary by scripture, not reason.

This passage marks a shift from divine attributes to prophetic attributes. The author clarifies that the obligatoriness of prophetic attributes is rooted in scriptural evidence, not rational proof. He also addresses differing scholarly views on how miracles confirm prophethood, with some considering it a rational proof while others see it as conventional or customary. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the foundations of prophetic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the transition
Notice that the author moves from discussing Allah's attributes to discussing the messengers' attributes. He introduces the obligatory attributes for messengers, giving them precedence due to their honor.
2
Define 'obligatoriness' in this context
The author defines wujūb as the impossibility of separation from the attribute in consideration of the Sharīʿah—meaning these attributes are necessary because scripture says so, not because reason demands them.
This is a key point: the basis is samʿī (scriptural), not ʿaqlī (rational).
3
Examine the three views on miracle evidence
The passage lists three scholarly opinions on how miracles support the claim to prophethood: wadʿī (positivistic), ʿādī (customary), and ʿaqlī (rational). Read each description carefully.
The author hints that considering it rational is questionable.
4
Clarify the phrase 'fī ḥaqqihim'
The commentator interprets 'fī ḥaqqihim' as 'with respect to their essence', and discusses whether the pronoun refers to messengers (rusul) or prophets (anbiyāʾ). Consider why the commentator might interpret it as prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَوَاجِبُ ... إلخ)، لمَّا تمَّمَ الكلام على ما يجب في حقِّهِ تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز، شرع في الكلام على ما يجب في حق الرسل وما يستحيل وما يجوز؛ مقدماً الواجب لشرفه .
والمراد بالوجوب هنا: عدم قبول الانفكاك بالنظر للشرع؛ لأن ما ذكر من الواجبات سمعي ، ولذا قال المصنف فيما يأتي : (ويستحيل ضدها كما رووا)، فأشار بذلك إلى أن استحالة ضدها بالدليل الشرعي؛ فيكون وجوبها بالدليل الشرعي . نعم؛ تصديق المعجزة لهم في دعوى الرسالة قيل : وضعي (٢) ؛ لتنزيلها منزلة الكلام، ودلالته وضعية، فكذا ما نزل منزلتَهُ . وقيل : عاديّ؛ لأنه بقرائنَ عادية (۳). وقيل : عقليّ ؛ لتنزهه تعالى عن تصديق الكاذب (٤) . وبذلك تعلم أن جعل الشارح الوجوب هنا عقليا فيه نظر.
وقوله : (فِي حَقِّهِم) أي: لذاتهم، فـ (في) بمعنى اللام ، و(حق) بمعنى الذات كما تقدَّم . والمتبادر من كلام المصنف أن الضمير عائد على الرسل، وفسَّرَهُ الشارح بالأنبياء قائلاً: (لأن معظم هذه الأحكام لا يختص بالرسل (٥) ، وكأن الشارح أشار إلى استخدام في المتن وإلا فالسابق في كلامه الرسل، ومراده بمعظم هذه الأحكام:
His saying: (And obligatory ... etc.), when he completed the discussion on what is obligatory with respect to Him, Most High, and what is impossible and what is permissible, he began discussion on what is obligatory with respect to the messengers, and what is impossible and what is permissible; giving precedence to the obligatory due to its honor.
And what is meant by obligatoriness here: the impossibility of separation [from the attribute] in consideration of the [revealed] Law; because what has been mentioned of obligations is scriptural (sam'i). That is why the author says later: (And the opposite of them is impossible as they have narrated), thereby indicating that the impossibility of their opposite is by scriptural evidence; thus their obligatoriness is by scriptural evidence. Yes; the confirmation of the miracle for them in the claim of prophethood is said to be: positivistic (wad'i) – because it is treated like speech, and its indication is positivistic, and similarly what is treated like it. And it is said: customary ('adi) – because it is by customary indications. And it is said: rational ('aqli) – because of the Most High being transcendent above confirming a liar. And by that you know that the commentator's making the obligatoriness here rational is subject to consideration.
And his saying: (with respect to them) means: with respect to their essence, so 'fi' is in the meaning of 'li' (for), and 'haqq' is in the meaning of 'dhat' (essence) as has preceded. And what is apparent from the author's speech is that the pronoun refers to the messengers, but the commentator interpreted it as [referring to] the prophets, saying: 'Because most of these rulings are not specific to messengers.' It is as if the commentator was alluding to usage in the text, otherwise the preceding [context] in his speech is 'messengers', and his intention by 'most of these rulings':
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a transition from the discussion of divine attributes to the discussion of prophetic attributes. The author explains that the obligatoriness (wujub) of these attributes for the prophets is understood scripturally (sam'i), not rationally. The evidence for their truthfulness is the miracle (mu'jiza), but scholars differ on the nature of that evidence: some say it is a positivist sign (wad'i) like speech, some say it is customary ('adi) based on circumstantial evidence, and some say it is rational ('aqli) because God would not confirm a liar. The commentator notes that considering the obligation rational is debatable. Finally, the phrase 'في حقهم' is clarified: 'في' has the sense of 'li' (for), and 'حق' means 'ذات' (essence). The pronoun 'them' is taken by the author as referring to messengers, but the commentator interprets it as prophets, arguing that most of these rulings apply to all prophets, not just messengers.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the obligatoriness of prophetic attributes is scriptural (samʿī), not rational. The necessity is derived from revelation, not pure reason.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of prophetic attributes is derived from scripture (samʿī), not reason, and the evidentiary value of miracles is interpreted differently by scholars as positivistic, customary, or rational.

Obligation of Prophets: Scriptural, Not Rational
Commentator defines wujub as scriptural necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obligation (wujūb) attributed to prophets in this context is based on scriptural evidence (samʿī), not on rational necessity.

Core Idea
Prophets' obligations are proven by revelation, not reason.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a key methodological point about the term 'obligation' when applied to prophets. Many assume that the impossibility of prophets sinning is a rational necessity, but here we learn that it is established through scriptural proof. This distinction is crucial for understanding the foundations of prophetic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the key terms: الوجوب, سمعي, الاستحالة, الدليل الشرعي.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the definition of obligation
The commentator defines wujūb here as 'non-acceptance of dissociation with regard to the Law.' This means that the prophets cannot be separated from these attributes, but this is based on revelation, not reason.
This is a technical definition.
3
Identify the scriptural basis
The commentator explains that the impossibility of the opposite (e.g., prophets sinning) is established by scriptural proof (الدليل الشرعي), so obligation is also scriptural.
Connect to the author's later line: 'And it is impossible for their opposite, as they narrated.'
4
Reflect on the common misconception
One might think that the impossibility of prophets sinning is rationally necessary. This passage guards against that error by clarifying it is based on revelation.
Why does this matter? It affects how we argue about prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بالوجوب هنا: عدم قبول الانفكاك بالنظر للشرع؛ لأن ما ذكر من الواجبات سمعي ، ولذا قال المصنف فيما يأتي : (ويستحيل ضدها كما رووا)، فأشار بذلك إلى أن استحالة ضدها بالدليل الشرعي؛ فيكون وجوبها بالدليل الشرعي

Hence the author says later: 'And it is impossible for their opposite, as they narrated.' Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'obligation' (wujūb) as it is used in the context of the prophets. The commentator clarifies that here it does not mean rational necessity but rather the impossibility of disjunction from the perspective of the Law (sharʿ). The reason is that all the obligations mentioned in this context are scriptural (samʿī), derived from revelation, not from reason. The author of the poem (al-Laqānī) indicates this by saying later: 'And it is impossible for their opposite, as they narrated.' The commentator explains that this impossibility is established by scriptural evidence, so the obligation itself is also established by scriptural evidence. This guards against the misunderstanding that these obligations are rationally necessary.

Three Views on the Indicative Basis of Miracles
Commentator lists three views on the basis of miracles indicating truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on how a miracle indicates the truthfulness of a prophet, and why the commentator questions making the obligation purely rational.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truthfulness, but how? Scholars differ: conventional, customary, or rational.

When a prophet performs a miracle, we infer that God is confirming his truthfulness. But what is the logical basis of that inference? The commentator presents three views: conventional (like speech), customary (by circumstantial indicators), and rational (because God would not support a liar). He then notes a flaw in the commentator's claim that the obligation is rational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three opinions
Read the Arabic passage and find the three opinions: conventional, customary, and rational. Notice how each is briefly justified.
The passage also includes a critical remark at the end.
2
Understand the first opinion: conventional (waḍʿī)
The first view treats the miracle like speech (kalām) whose meaning is conventionally assigned. Just as speech indicates its meaning by convention, the miracle indicates truthfulness by a similar conventional relation.
This view compares the miracle to a divine statement.
3
Understand the second and third opinions, and the critique
The second view says the indication is customary, based on the usual circumstances accompanying miracles. The third says it is rational, because God would not confirm a liar. The commentator then notes that making the obligation rational is questionable.
The critique suggests the basis is scriptural, not rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم؛ تصديق المعجزة لهم في دعوى الرسالة قيل : وضعي ؛ لتنزيلها منزلة الكلام، ودلالته وضعية، فكذا ما نزل منزلتَهُ . وقيل : عاديّ؛ لأنه بقرائنَ عادية . وقيل : عقليّ ؛ لتنزهه تعالى عن تصديق الكاذب . وبذلك تعلم أن جعل الشارح الوجوب هنا عقليا فيه نظر.
Yes; the affirmation of the miracle for them concerning the claim of messengership: it was said: conventional (waḍʿī), because it is treated as speech, and its indication is conventional, and likewise what is treated as such. And it was said: customary (ʿādī), because it is by customary indicators. And it was said: rational (ʿaqlī), because of His transcendence – may He be exalted – from affirming a liar. And by that you know that the commentator's making the obligation here rational is questionable.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents three scholarly opinions on the basis by which a miracle (muʿjiza) indicates the truthfulness of a prophet's claim to messengership. The first view is that the indication is conventional (waḍʿī), meaning it is like speech (kalām) whose meaning is conventionally assigned; the miracle is treated as if God says 'My servant is truthful.' The second view is that it is customary (ʿādī), because the miracle occurs accompanied by customary circumstances (qarāʾin ʿādiyya) that indicate the prophet's truth. The third view is that it is rational (ʿaqlī), because God is too exalted to corroborate a liar. The commentator then notes that from this discussion, one can see that the claim of the commentator (al-Shārīḥ) that the obligation here is rational is questionable, implying it is actually scriptural.

Key Terms
المعجزة
وضعي
عادي
عقلي
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the miracle's indication is purely rational.
Correction: The commentator shows it is debated and that the obligation to believe the prophet is ultimately based on revelation, not pure reason.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the opinions on how a miracle indicates truthfulness?
A) Conventional (waḍʿī)
B) Customary (ʿādī)
C) Rational (ʿaqlī)
D) Legal (sharʿī)

Key Takeaway
The basis of a miracle's indication of truthfulness is debated among scholars: it is either conventional, customary, or rational, but the commentator questions the rational view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle, do you assume it automatically proves the prophet's truth, or do you stop to consider the basis of that indication? How does this lesson challenge your default assumption?
?
The commentator questions the rational view. Are you willing to question what seems ‘logical’ to you about faith? What would it cost you to accept that the obligation is scriptural rather than rational?
?
If someone argued that miracles must be rationally verifiable, how would you use the three views from this lesson to respond? And what does your personal reliance on reason versus revelation reveal about your trust in God's wisdom?

The Scope of Prophetic Obligations: Messengers vs Prophets
Commentator explains the phrase 'fī ḥaqqihim' and the interpretive issue of its pronoun referent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the phrase 'fī ḥaqqihim' in the poem and the interpretive issue of whether it refers to messengers or all prophets, and the exception of the duty of delivery.

Core Idea
The pronoun in 'fī ḥaqqihim' can refer to prophets, not just messengers, except for the unique duty of delivery.

This short passage from the commentary clarifies a key phrase in the poem. The commentator first explains the literal meaning of 'fī ḥaqqihim' as 'for their own selves'. Then an important interpretive question arises: does the pronoun refer only to messengers or to all prophets? The commentator argues for the broader reference, with one crucial exception. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the literal meaning of the phrase
Read the first part of the passage where the commentator interprets 'fī ḥaqqihim' as 'for their selves' by equating 'fī' with 'lām' and 'ḥaqq' with 'dhāt'.
This sets the linguistic foundation.
2
Identify the interpretive issue: messengers vs prophets
Notice that the apparent antecedent of the pronoun is 'messengers', but the commentator interprets it as 'prophets' because most of the rulings are not exclusive to messengers. Look for the phrase 'most of these rulings' and see how the commentator justifies the shift.
The commentator suggests the author used a rhetorical device called istikhdām.
3
Note the exception: the duty of delivery
The commentator states that the duty of delivery (tablīgh) is specific to messengers. Read the final part where some scholars even generalize this duty to prophets for respect.
This exception highlights the unique role of messengers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (فِي حَقِّهِم) أي: لذاتهم، فـ (في) بمعنى اللام ، و(حق) بمعنى الذات كما تقدَّم والمتبادر من كلام المصنف أن الضمير عائد على الرسل، وفسَّرَهُ الشارح بالأنبياء قائلاً: (لأن معظم هذه الأحكام لا يختص بالرسل) ، وكأن الشارح أشار إلى استخدام في المتن وإلا فالسابق في كلامه الرسل، ومراده بمعظم هذه الأحكام: ما عدا التبليغ، فإن التبليغ خاص بالرسل، وبعضُهم عمَّمه للأنبياء؛ لأنه يجب على النبي أن يُبلغ أنه نبي ليُحترم .
And His statement: 'in their right' (fī ḥaqqihim) means: for their selves, so 'fī' is in the sense of 'lām', and 'ḥaqq' is in the sense of 'dhāt' (essence) as previously. And the apparent meaning from the author's speech is that the pronoun refers to the messengers (rusul). But the commentator (al-Shārīḥ) interpreted it as the prophets (anbiyā'), saying: 'Because most of these rulings are not specific to messengers.' And it is as if the commentator indicated a figurative usage (istikhdām) in the text; otherwise the preceding [mention] in his speech was 'messengers'. And his intention by 'most of these rulings' is: except for delivery (tablīgh), for delivery is specific to messengers, and some scholars generalized it to the prophets because it is obligatory for a prophet to announce that he is a prophet so that he may be respected.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'fī ḥaqqihim' from the poem. The commentator first clarifies the literal meaning: 'fī' here means 'for' (lām), and 'ḥaqq' means 'essence' (dhāt). So the phrase means 'for their own selves'. The apparent reference of the pronoun is to the messengers (rusul), as the author had been discussing messengers. However, the commentator (al-Shārīḥ) interprets it as including all prophets (anbiyā'), because most of the obligations mentioned are not exclusive to messengers. The commentator's interpretation involves a figure of speech (istikhdām) where the pronoun refers to both groups simultaneously, though the immediate antecedent is 'messengers'. The exception is the duty of delivering the revelation (tablīgh), which is specific to messengers. Some scholars even generalize this duty to prophets, arguing that a prophet must announce his prophethood so that people respect him.

Key Terms
حق
الرسل
الأنبياء
التبليغ
استخدام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all obligations of messengers apply equally to prophets.
Correction: The commentator distinguishes the duty of delivery (tablīgh) as specific to messengers, though some scholars generalize it.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the exception to the rule that most of the rulings apply to both messengers and prophets?
A) The obligation to perform miracles
B) The duty of delivery (tablīgh)
C) The duty to be sinless
D) The duty to convey the message to all people

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'fī ḥaqqihim' can refer to all prophets, not just messengers, with the notable exception of the duty of delivery (tablīgh), which is specific to messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'fī ḥaqqihim' (in their right), did you immediately assume it only applied to the 'special ones'—the messengers? Is there a part of you that uses such distinctions to excuse yourself from the demands of prophethood? What would change in your life if you saw every prophetic obligation (except tablīgh) as applying to you personally—and what are you going to do about that today?
?
The commentator insists that even a prophet must announce his prophethood so that he may be respected. Have you been hiding your faith or your role as a bearer of divine truth out of fear or humility? Where is the line between humility and cowardice in your own life? Will you commit to openly declaring, in your own capacity, what you know to be true—even if you are not a messenger?
?
The rhetorical device 'istikhdām' allows a word to carry two meanings at once. Are you living a double life—using one meaning for yourself and another for others? Ask yourself: In what areas of my worship or character do I apply a stricter standard to others than to myself, or vice versa? What would it look like to be 'for their own selves' in truth, with no gap between what you claim and what you are?

The Infallibility of Prophets: Definition and Scope
Commentator defines 'ismah as protection from all prohibitions, even before prophethood, and states prophets' actions are either obligatory or recommended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive definition of prophetic infallibility (ʿiṣmah) as covering all prohibitions, even before prophethood, and the classification of prophets' actions as either obligatory or recommended.

Core Idea
Prophets are protected from all sins, even before prophethood, and their actions are always acts of obedience.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Murīd explains the concept of 'al-amānah' (the trust) which is a key term for prophetic infallibility. The commentator clarifies that prophets are preserved from all prohibited things, outward and inward, and that this protection applies even before prophethood. He also discusses the nature of their actions, which are always either obligatory or recommended, never merely permissible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: al-amānah, al-munhā 'anhu, karāha, khilāf al-awlā, tashrīʿ, wājib, mandūb.
2
Identify the definition of 'ismah
Find where the commentator defines al-amānah and note what it covers: outward and inward prohibitions, even before prophethood and in childhood.
3
Understand the scope of protection
The commentator says prophets are protected from makrūh, khilāf al-awlā, and even mubāḥ if it would be considered such. Note the exception for legislation.
4
Learn the classification of prophetic actions
The passage states that prophets' actions are either wājib or mandūb. Understand why they never do purely permissible actions.
5
Reflect on the broader application
The commentator mentions that awliyā' can reach a station where their actions become obedience through intentions. This connects to the prophetic model.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الَامَانَهُ) بالنقل والدرج للوزن؛ وهي حفظ ظواهرهم وبواطنهم من التلبس من منهي عنه ولو نهي كراهة أو خلاف الأولى فهم محفوظون ظاهراً من الزنا وشرب الخمر والكذب وغير ذلك من منهيات الظاهر، ومحفوظون باطناً من الحسد والكبر والرياء، وغير ذلك من منهيات الباطن والمراد : المنهى عنه ، : ولو صورةً، فيشمل ما قبل النبوة ولو في حال الصغر ولا يقعُ منهم مكروه ولا خلاف الأولى، بل ولا مباح على وجه كونه مكروهاً أو خلاف الأولى أو مباحاً، وإذا وقع صورة ذلك فهو للتشريع؛ فيصير واجباً أو مندوباً في حقهم، فأفعالهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام دائرة بين الواجب والمندوب، بل في الأولياء الذين هم أتباعُهم من يصل لمقام تصير حركاته وسكناته طاعات بالنيَّات .
His statement: 'al-amānah' (the trust) by way of transposition and elision for the meter; and it is the preservation of their outward and inward [aspects] from being tainted by anything prohibited, even if the prohibition is of dislike (karāha) or contrary to the better (khilāf al-awlā). So they are preserved outwardly from adultery, drinking wine, lying, and other outward prohibitions. And they are preserved inwardly from envy, pride, hypocrisy, and other inward prohibitions. And the intended meaning is: the prohibited thing, even in form, so it includes [the period] before prophethood, even in the state of childhood. And nothing makrūh or khilāf al-awlā occurs from them, nor even something mubāḥ in the manner of being makrūh or khilāf al-awlā or mubāḥ. And if such an action occurs in form, it is for legislation (tashrīʿ); so it becomes obligatory (wājib) or recommended (mandūb) in their right. Thus their actions – upon them be prayer and peace – revolve between the obligatory and the recommended. Even among the awliyā' who are their followers, there is one who reaches a station where his movements and stillness become acts of obedience by [good] intentions.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-amānah' (the trust) in the context of prophetic infallibility (ʿiṣmah). The commentator explains that it means God's preservation of the prophets from being tainted by any prohibited thing, whether the prohibition is definitive (ḥarām) or based on dislike (karāha) or even contrary to the better (khilāf al-awlā). This preservation covers both outward actions (like adultery, drinking, lying) and inward states (like envy, pride, hypocrisy). Moreover, this preservation applies even before prophethood and even in childhood. No action that is makrūh (disliked) or khilāf al-awlā occurs from them, nor even an action that is mubāḥ (permissible) if it would be considered makrūh or khilāf al-awlā. If an action that appears to be such occurs, it is for the purpose of legislation (tashrīʿ), meaning they do it as an example, and thus it becomes obligatory or recommended for them. Therefore, the actions of prophets are always either obligatory or recommended; they never engage in permissible acts in the usual sense. The commentator adds that among the awliyā' (saints) who follow the prophets, there are those who reach such a station that all their movements and stillness become acts of worship through their intentions.

Key Terms
الأمانة
العصمة
المنهي عنه
الكراهة
خلاف الأولى
التشريع
واجب
مندوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets could commit minor sins before prophethood.
Correction: The commentator states that al-amānah includes protection from all prohibited things even before prophethood and in childhood.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what happens if a prophet appears to do an action that would normally be considered mubāḥ (permissible)?
A) It is actually a sin for them.
B) It is for legislation and becomes wajib or mandub for them.
C) It remains mubāḥ for them.
D) It is makrūh for them.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic infallibility is comprehensive, covering all prohibitions before and during prophethood, and prophets' actions are always either obligatory or recommended, not merely permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator says that even the awliyā' can reach a station where every movement becomes worship through intention. What is one mundane, permissible action in your daily life that you could transform into an act of obedience with a sincere intention? Will you do it starting today?
?
Prophets never commit even a makruh or khilaf al-awla action. What is one disliked habit you tolerate in yourself (e.g., wasting time, harsh words) that you need to abandon to draw closer to their example? What concrete step will you take to leave it this week?
?
If prophets’ actions are always wajib or mandub, what does that say about your own attitude toward time and deeds? Are you content with a life where many of your hours are spent in mubah (permissible) activities, not turned into worship? What change in your daily routine can you make today to raise the bar?

Prophetic Actions and the Objection of Disliked Acts
Sanusi repels an objection by invoking the legislative nature of prophetic actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of prophetic infallibility resolves apparent makruh actions performed by the Prophet.

Core Idea
Every prophetic action is either legislative or indicative, never truly disliked.

When we hear that the Prophet performed actions like urinating while standing or doing wudu once, we might wonder how these can be from an infallible prophet. Sanusi addresses this objection directly, showing that such acts are repelled by the preceding explanation of prophetic acts being for legislation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Look at the list of reported actions: performing wudu once and twice, urinating while standing, drinking while standing. Understand that these actions might seem problematic for a prophet.
These actions are considered makruh or khilaf al-awla for ordinary Muslims.
2
Understand 'with this is repelled'
The phrase 'with this' refers back to the earlier principle that any action of a prophet that appears makruh is actually for legislation (tashri) and thus becomes wajib or mandub for him.
This principle preserves the prophet's infallibility.
3
Connect to infallibility
Realize that these actions do not contradict 'ismah because their purpose is to teach or legislate, not to commit a disliked act.
4
Reflect on the defense
The objection is repelled by recontextualizing the actions as legislative, showing that Sanusi's framework is coherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا اندفعَ ما يُقالُ : قد ثبت أنه توضا مرَّةً مرَّة (١)، ومرتين مرتين (۲)، وبال مرتين، قائماً (۳) ، وشرب قائماً (٤) .
And with this is repelled that which is said: It has been established that he performed wudu once each (once), and twice each (twice), and urinated twice while standing, and drank while standing.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents an objection that is repelled by the preceding explanation of prophetic infallibility. The objection lists certain actions reported from the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) that might seem to be makruh or khilaf al-awla for ordinary people: performing wudu with one washing each part (once), doing wudu twice each, urinating while standing, and drinking while standing. The phrase 'by this is repelled' refers back to the principle established in the previous lesson: that any such action from a prophet, if it appears formally as makruh or khilaf al-awla, is actually for legislation and thus becomes wajib or mandub in their case. Therefore, these actions are not contrary to their infallibility.

Key Terms
اندفع
توضا مرة مرة
بال قائماً
شرب قائماً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these actions contradict the prophet's infallibility because they appear disliked for ordinary people.
Correction: The actions are actually legislative acts that become obligatory or recommended for the prophet, so they do not violate infallibility.

Checkpoint
How does the passage respond to the objection about the prophet doing wudu once and twice, etc.?
A) It denies that these actions ever occurred
B) It says these actions were abrogated
C) It says the preceding explanation of infallibility repels the objection because such acts are for legislation
D) It says these actions were mistakes

Key Takeaway
Apparent disliked acts of the Prophet are actually legislative and thus consistent with infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If prophetic actions are legislative and establish rulings, how should that affect the way you seek to emulate the Prophet in your daily life? Will you now pause to ask whether a certain action was legislative for him or a general sunnah for you?

Prophetic Infallibility from Forbidden Acts and Interpretation of Apparent Sins
Explains prophetic infallibility from haram and interprets apparent sins through inward command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Sunni theology maintains prophetic infallibility by interpreting actions that appear sinful as matters of inward command and higher spiritual principle.

Core Idea
What looks like a prophet's sin is actually a secret obedience.

This passage addresses a crucial point in prophetic infallibility: the status of actions that appear to be sins. How can we reconcile the prophets' sinlessness with events like Adam eating from the tree? The author explains that prophets are infallible from all forbidden acts by consensus, and any apparent transgression must be interpreted according to a profound spiritual principle. Let's explore this teaching.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim of infallibility from haram
The passage begins by asserting that prophets never commit forbidden acts by consensus. Notice the absolute nature of this claim.
2
Learn the interpretive principle
The principle 'hasanat al-abrar sayyi'at al-muqarrabin' is introduced. Consider what this implies about the relativity of actions.
3
Examine Adam's case
The passage explains Adam's action as a sin unlike other sins, due to an inward command. Reflect on the concept of ta'wil.
4
Apply to other narratives
The same principle applies to the brothers of Joseph. See how this protects the doctrine of infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المحرَّم فلم يقع منهم إجماعاً، وما أوهم المعصية فمؤوّل بأنه من باب: حسنات الأبرار (٥) سيئات المقربين ولا يجوز النطقُ به في غير مورده إلا في مقام البيان، وما وقع من آدم فهو معصيةٌ لا كالمعاصي ؛ لأنَّه تأوَّل الأمر لسر بينه وبين سيده ، وإن لم نعلمه (٦) ، حتى نقل في «اليواقيت» عن أبي مدين : (لو كنت بدل آدم لأكلت الشجرة بتمامها ) (٧) ، فهو - وإن كان منهيًّا عنه ظاهراً - مأمورٌ باطناً، وكذا يُقالُ فيما وقع من إخوة يوسف على القول بأنهم أنبياء (۸) .
As for the forbidden (al-muharram), it has not occurred from them by consensus. And what appears to imply sin is interpreted as being from the category: 'The good deeds of the pious are the sins of those brought near.' And it is not permissible to utter it outside its context except in a context of explanation. And what occurred from Adam is a sin unlike other sins, because he interpreted the command due to a secret between him and his Lord, even if we do not know it. Until it is narrated in 'al-Yawaqit' from Abu Madyan: 'If I were in place of Adam, I would have eaten the whole tree.' So it is, although it was outwardly prohibited, inwardly commanded. And similarly is said regarding what occurred from the brothers of Joseph on the view that they were prophets.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage clarifies that prophets are completely infallible from haram (forbidden acts) by consensus. Any action that might appear to involve disobedience is interpreted according to the principle 'hasanat al-abrar sayyi'at al-muqarrabin' (the good deeds of the pious are the sins of those brought near), meaning that what is a great good deed for ordinary people might be a lesser shortcoming for the elite. It is forbidden to speak about such matters except in contexts of explanation. The specific case of Adam (peace be upon him) is addressed: his action was a sin but not like ordinary sins; he misinterpreted the command due to a secret between him and his Lord that we may not know. The quote from Abu Madyan illustrates that if he were in Adam's place, he would have eaten the whole tree, suggesting that Adam's action was inwardly commanded. The same principle applies to the brothers of Joseph on the view that they were prophets. This underscores the complete protection of prophets from actual disobedience.

Key Terms
المحرَّم
حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين
التأويل
معصية
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adam's sin proves that prophets can commit sins.
Correction: The passage explains that Adam's action was a sin unlike other sins due to an inward command, preserving infallibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how should we understand Adam's action of eating from the tree?
A) It was a major sin that he repented from
B) It was not a sin at all but a permitted act
C) It was a sin unlike other sins because he interpreted the command due to a secret between him and his Lord
D) It was a mistake that he made unintentionally

Key Takeaway
Prophetic infallibility is absolute regarding forbidden acts; any apparent sin is interpreted as an inwardly commanded action due to a higher secret between the prophet and Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you felt you sinned or failed. Could it be that Allah had a secret command for you in that moment, hidden from your understanding? How would viewing that event as an inwardly commanded obedience change your relationship with your past?
?
The principle 'the good deeds of the pious are the sins of the near ones' suggests that spiritual growth raises the standard. Are you satisfied with being an ordinary believer, or do you long for the station where even your 'good deeds' are scrutinized? What step will you take today to move closer to Allah?
?
Abu Madyan said he would have eaten the whole tree if he were Adam, trusting the inward command over the outward prohibition. Is there an area of your life where you are holding back from full surrender out of fear of appearing sinful? How can you cultivate that level of trust in Allah's hidden wisdom?

The Good Deeds of the Righteous Are the Sins of the Near Ones
The author refutes objections and introduces the principle with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that explains seemingly sinful actions of prophets and how it applies to Adam and Joseph's brothers.

Core Idea
What seems sinful in prophets is actually a higher station's shortcoming.

This passage addresses a key question: if prophets are sinless, how do we understand reports that seem to show them committing sins? The author introduces a profound principle that actions considered virtuous for ordinary people may be considered less than ideal for prophets due to their closeness to Allah. This lesson unpacks that principle and its application to specific cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's refutation
Read the opening where the author lists objections about prophetic actions like wudu and urination. Note that these are not actual sins.
2
Understand the principle
Focus on the statement: 'وما أوهم المعصية فمؤوّل بأنه من باب: حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين'. This is the key interpretive principle.
This principle is central to reconciling apparent flaws with sinlessness.
3
Examine the examples
Read how Adam's action is described as a 'sin unlike other sins' due to an inner command, and similarly for Joseph's brothers.
Notice the quote from Abu Madyan about Adam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا اندفعَ ما يُقالُ : قد ثبت أنه توضا مرَّةٌ مرَّة (١)، ومرتين مرتين (٢)، وبال مرتين، قائماً (٣) ، وشرب قائماً (٤) .
وأما المحرَّم فلم يقع منهم إجماعاً، وما أوهم المعصية فمؤوّل بأنه من باب: حسنات الأبرار (٥) سيئات المقربين ولا يجوز النطقُ به في غير مورده إلا في مقام البيان، وما وقع من آدم فهو معصيةٌ لا كالمعاصي ; لأنَّه تأوَّل الأمر لسر بينه وبين سيده ، وإن لم نعلمه (٦) ، حتى نقل في «اليواقيت» عن أبي مدين : (لو كنت بدل آدم لأكلت الشجرة بتمامها ) ، فهو - وإن كان منهيًّا عنه ظاهراً - مأمورٌ باطناً، وكذا يُقالُ فيما وقع من إخوة يوسف على القول بأنهم أنبياء (۸) .
فليحذر العالم وغيره من الحديث بالآيات والآثار المتشابهات في حقّ الأنبياء كحذره عنها في حقه تعالى; إذ أذهان المتشرعين فضلاً عن العامة كثيراً ما يسبق إليها نسبة المعصية استسلاماً لظواهر النصوص، ومن هنا ينشأ الحرمان بإسقاط تعظيم الأنبياء عموماً ، فضلاً عن سيدهم عليه وعليهم أزكى الصلوات والتسليمات; قال العلامة الأمير في حاشيته (ص) (١١٤): (وما أوهم معصية لا يجوز النطق به في غير مورده إلا للبيان وأصله : حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين ; فآدم تأوّل، أو له في ذلك مع سيده سر وإن لم نعلمه) .
And by this is repelled what is said: It is established that he performed wudu once (1), and twice (2), and urinated twice while standing (3), and drank while standing (4). As for the forbidden, it did not occur from them unanimously, and whatever suggests sin is interpreted as being from the category: 'The good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones' and it is not permissible to utter it outside its context except in a position of explanation. And what occurred from Adam was a sin unlike other sins, because he interpreted the command for a secret between him and his Lord, even if we do not know it (6), until it was narrated in 'al-Yawaqit' from Abu Madyan: 'If I were in Adam's place, I would have eaten the entire tree' – so he, although outwardly forbidden, was inwardly commanded. And similarly is said regarding what occurred from Joseph's brothers on the view that they were prophets (8). So let the scholar and others beware of speaking about the ambiguous verses and reports concerning the prophets, just as they are wary of them concerning Him, the Exalted; for the minds of the religiously learned, let alone the common people, often leap to attributing sin to them, surrendering to the apparent meanings of the texts, and from this arises deprivation by dropping the veneration of the prophets in general, let alone their master (peace and blessings be upon him and them). Al-Allama al-Amir said in his gloss (p. 114): 'What suggests sin is not permissible to utter outside its context except for explanation; its origin is: the good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones. So Adam interpreted, or there is for him in that a secret with his Lord, even if we do not know it.'
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and explanation. The author first responds to objections based on hadiths that prophets did certain actions (like wudu once or twice, etc.). He then states that actual sin (المحرم) never occurred from prophets unanimously, and whatever seems to suggest sin is interpreted based on the principle that 'the good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones.' Adam's action was a sin unlike others because he misinterpreted a command due to a secret between him and his Lord. Then he quotes a saying from Abu Madyan that if he were Adam, he would have eaten the whole tree, indicating that Adam was commanded inwardly. Similarly for Joseph's brothers if they were prophets. Then he warns scholars and others to be cautious when speaking about ambiguous texts concerning prophets, as the minds of people may slip into attributing sin. He ends with a quote from Al-Ameer affirming the principle and that there may be a secret involved.

Key Terms
حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين
التأويل
المعصية
الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets can commit actual sins.
Correction: Their actions that appear sinful are either not forbidden or are interpreted as a higher station's shortcoming, as per the principle 'حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what principle explains actions of prophets that seem sinful?
A) They are actually sins but forgiven.
B) The good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones.
C) Prophets are not bound by divine commands.
D) They were done before prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The principle of 'حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين' protects the integrity of prophetic sinlessness while accounting for apparent shortcomings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone make a mistake that you consider a sin, do you instinctively judge them, or do you pause to consider that their station with Allah might be higher than yours, and what they did may not be a sin at all? How will you change your reaction starting today?
?
Have you ever considered that your own 'good deeds' might be a source of pride and thus a sin in the eyes of those closer to Allah? What are the deeds you feel most proud of, and how can you purify your intention so they remain good deeds?
?
If you were in Adam's position, would you have obeyed outwardly or sought the deeper command? What does your answer reveal about your relationship with Allah's hidden wisdom—are you content with not knowing every secret?

Beware of Ambiguous Texts Concerning the Prophets
The author warns about ambiguous texts and quotes al-Amir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of caution when interpreting ambiguous verses and reports about the prophets, and the danger of attributing sin to them.

Core Idea
Speak cautiously about prophets; their apparent flaws have hidden wisdom.

The author concludes with a powerful warning: both scholars and laypeople must be extremely careful when discussing ambiguous texts about the prophets. Just as we avoid anthropomorphic interpretations about Allah, we must avoid attributing sin to the prophets based on surface meanings. This lesson highlights that caution and the consequence of losing veneration.

Guided Path
1
Read the warning
Read the passage starting from 'فليحذر العالم...' and note the comparison to caution about Allah's attributes.
2
Understand the reason
Note why caution is needed: people's minds jump to attributing sin when they see apparent meanings.
3
Identify the consequence
Notice the consequence: deprivation by dropping the veneration of the prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا اندفعَ ما يُقالُ : قد ثبت أنه توضا مرَّةٌ مرَّة (١)، ومرتين مرتين (٢)، وبال مرتين، قائماً (٣) ، وشرب قائماً (٤) .
وأما المحرَّم فلم يقع منهم إجماعاً، وما أوهم المعصية فمؤوّل بأنه من باب: حسنات الأبرار (٥) سيئات المقربين ولا يجوز النطقُ به في غير مورده إلا في مقام البيان، وما وقع من آدم فهو معصيةٌ لا كالمعاصي ; لأنَّه تأوَّل الأمر لسر بينه وبين سيده ، وإن لم نعلمه (٦) ، حتى نقل في «اليواقيت» عن أبي مدين : (لو كنت بدل آدم لأكلت الشجرة بتمامها ) ، فهو - وإن كان منهيًّا عنه ظاهراً - مأمورٌ باطناً، وكذا يُقالُ فيما وقع من إخوة يوسف على القول بأنهم أنبياء (۸) .
فليحذر العالم وغيره من الحديث بالآيات والآثار المتشابهات في حقّ الأنبياء كحذره عنها في حقه تعالى; إذ أذهان المتشرعين فضلاً عن العامة كثيراً ما يسبق إليها نسبة المعصية استسلاماً لظواهر النصوص، ومن هنا ينشأ الحرمان بإسقاط تعظيم الأنبياء عموماً ، فضلاً عن سيدهم عليه وعليهم أزكى الصلوات والتسليمات; قال العلامة الأمير في حاشيته (ص) (١١٤): (وما أوهم معصية لا يجوز النطق به في غير مورده إلا للبيان وأصله : حسنات الأبرار سيئات المقربين ; فآدم تأوّل، أو له في ذلك مع سيده سر وإن لم نعلمه) .
And by this is repelled what is said: It is established that he performed wudu once (1), and twice (2), and urinated twice while standing (3), and drank while standing (4). As for the forbidden, it did not occur from them unanimously, and whatever suggests sin is interpreted as being from the category: 'The good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones' and it is not permissible to utter it outside its context except in a position of explanation. And what occurred from Adam was a sin unlike other sins, because he interpreted the command for a secret between him and his Lord, even if we do not know it (6), until it was narrated in 'al-Yawaqit' from Abu Madyan: 'If I were in Adam's place, I would have eaten the entire tree' – so he, although outwardly forbidden, was inwardly commanded. And similarly is said regarding what occurred from Joseph's brothers on the view that they were prophets (8). So let the scholar and others beware of speaking about the ambiguous verses and reports concerning the prophets, just as they are wary of them concerning Him, the Exalted; for the minds of the religiously learned, let alone the common people, often leap to attributing sin to them, surrendering to the apparent meanings of the texts, and from this arises deprivation by dropping the veneration of the prophets in general, let alone their master (peace and blessings be upon him and them). Al-Allama al-Amir said in his gloss (p. 114): 'What suggests sin is not permissible to utter outside its context except for explanation; its origin is: the good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones. So Adam interpreted, or there is for him in that a secret with his Lord, even if we do not know it.'
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and explanation. The author first responds to objections based on hadiths that prophets did certain actions (like wudu once or twice, etc.). He then states that actual sin (المحرم) never occurred from prophets unanimously, and whatever seems to suggest sin is interpreted based on the principle that 'the good deeds of the righteous are the sins of the near ones.' Adam's action was a sin unlike others because he misinterpreted a command due to a secret between him and his Lord. Then he quotes a saying from Abu Madyan that if he were Adam, he would have eaten the whole tree, indicating that Adam was commanded inwardly. Similarly for Joseph's brothers if they were prophets. Then he warns scholars and others to be cautious when speaking about ambiguous texts concerning prophets, as the minds of people may slip into attributing sin. He ends with a quote from Al-Ameer affirming the principle and that there may be a secret involved.

Key Terms
المتشابهات
تعظيم الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to freely discuss ambiguous texts about prophets without caution.
Correction: One must be extremely cautious, as attributing sin to prophets based on apparent meanings leads to loss of veneration and spiritual deprivation.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say is the consequence of not being cautious about ambiguous texts concerning prophets?
A) It leads to innovation.
B) It leads to deprivation by dropping the veneration of the prophets.
C) It leads to disbelief.
D) It leads to misinterpreting the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Treat ambiguous texts about prophets with the same caution as those about Allah to preserve proper veneration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Quranic story where a prophet seems to make a mistake, do you feel a subtle judgment in your heart? Will you repent from that and affirm that there is a secret with Allah you do not know?
?
Have you ever repeated an ambiguous story about a prophet without proper caution, perhaps causing others to lose veneration? How will you guard your tongue from now on?
?
The author says losing veneration of prophets leads to deprivation. Examine your own heart: do you truly hold the prophets in the highest esteem, or have you allowed doubts from ambiguous texts to creep in? What will you actively do to strengthen your reverence?

The Proof for Trustworthiness in Prophets
Sanusi presents a proof for prophetic trustworthiness and clarifies its legal basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that prophets must be trustworthy and why it is considered a legal rather than purely rational argument.

Core Idea
If prophets could betray, following them would require us to sin—but Allah never commands sin.

This lesson examines a key proof from Sanusi establishing that prophets are inherently trustworthy. The proof uses a conditional argument based on the divine command to follow prophets. It also addresses the nature of the proof, clarifying that it is not purely rational but rooted in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the conditional structure: if prophets betray, then we would be commanded to follow them in that act.
Pay attention to the three categories of acts mentioned.
2
Identify the three act categories
Note the three types of acts that are excluded from prophets: forbidden (حرام), disliked (مكروه), and contrary to the better choice (خلاف الأولى).
3
Understand the proof's nature
Consider why the author says this proof is a legal proof (دليل شرعي) despite its rational appearance. The key is that the premises come from revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل وجوبِ الأمانة لهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام : أنَّهم لو خانوا بفعل محرَّمِ أو مكروه أو خلافِ الأولى، لكنَّا مأمورين به؛ لأنَّ الله تعالى أمرنا باتباعهم في أقوالهم وأفعالهم وأحوالهم من غير تفصيل، وهو تعالى لا يأمر بمحرم ولا مكروه ولا خلاف الأولى، فلا تكون أفعالهم محرمة ولا مكروهةً ولا خلاف الأولى، وهذا الدليل وإن كان على صورة الدليل العقلي هو في الحقيقة دليل شرعي ; لأنَّ دليل الملازمة شرعي، وبطلان التالي بدليل شرعي ; وهو أن الله لا يأمر بالفحشاء.
And the proof for the obligation of trustworthiness for them (peace and blessings be upon them) is that if they were to betray by committing a forbidden act, or a disliked act, or an act contrary to the better choice, then we would be commanded to do it; because Allah the Exalted has commanded us to follow them in their sayings, actions, and states without specification, and He, the Exalted, does not command a forbidden act, nor a disliked act, nor an act contrary to the better choice. So their actions are not forbidden, nor disliked, nor contrary to the better choice. And this proof, even if it appears in the form of a rational proof, is in reality a legal proof, because the premise of the implication is legal, and the falsity of the consequent is by a legal proof, which is that Allah does not command indecency.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the obligation of trustworthiness (الأمانة) for prophets. The proof is structured as a conditional: If prophets were to betray (commit a forbidden, disliked, or less preferred act), then we would be commanded to do that act, because Allah has commanded us to follow them in all sayings, actions, and states without exception. But Allah does not command any forbidden, disliked, or less preferred act. Therefore, their actions are free from such. The author notes that although the proof appears rational, it is actually a legal proof because the premise of the implication (that we are commanded to follow them) is from revelation, and the negation of the consequent (that Allah does not command evil) is also from revelation.

Key Terms
الأمانة
محرم
مكروه
خلاف الأولى
دليل عقلي
دليل شرعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is a purely rational proof (دليل عقلي) because it uses logical conditional reasoning.
Correction: The author clarifies that although it appears rational, it is actually a legal proof (دليل شرعي) because its premises (the command to follow prophets and that Allah does not command evil) come from revelation, not pure reason.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why can prophets not commit forbidden acts?
A) Because they are naturally perfect.
B) Because Allah commands us to follow them and He does not command evil.
C) Because they would lose their status.
D) Because they have no free will.

Key Takeaway
The prophet's trustworthiness is proven by the fact that following them would otherwise entail obeying commands to sin, which is impossible from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from the Prophet ﷺ that goes against your desires, do you truly believe he could never betray you, or do you quietly assume the command might be negotiable? How does this proof expose the inconsistency in your obedience?
?
Reflect on a specific instance where you chose an easier, less preferred action over a stronger Sunnah (e.g., praying in a less preferred way or skipping a recommended fast). In light of this lesson, does that choice reflect a subtle doubt about the Prophet's trustworthiness? What will you change starting today?
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and He asked you, 'Did you follow My Prophet without exception?', what would He find in your heart? Does this lesson not demand that you stop excusing your selective obedience and commit to unconditional emulation?

Defining Prophetic Truthfulness and Addressing an Objection
Defines truthfulness and answers an objection via the concept of insha'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars define prophetic truthfulness and how they answer the objection from the palm tree incident.

Core Idea
Truthfulness is correspondence with reality; expressions of hope are not reports.

The author begins by defining prophetic truthfulness as the correspondence of their reports with reality, even if only according to their belief. He then addresses a potential objection: the Prophet's statement to palm tree growers, which seems to be a false prediction. The answer clarifies that this statement was a construction (insha'), an expression of hope, not a factual report, and thus not subject to truth or falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of truthfulness
Read the Arabic definition carefully: truthfulness is the correspondence of the report with reality, even if according to their belief. Note that the Prophet's words 'All that did not happen' are an example of truthfulness according to his belief.
2
Study the objection and answer
Read the objection about the palm tree incident. The answer explains that the Prophet's statement was a construction (insha'), not a report, so it is not evaluated as true or false.
Understand the distinction between reports (khabar) and constructions (insha').
3
Reflect on why the objection fails
Consider why the non-occurrence of the hoped outcome does not harm the Prophet's truthfulness. The key is that the statement was not a claim about reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَصِدْقُهُمْ) معطوف على الأمانة; أي: وواجبٌ في حقهم صدقهم; وهو مطابقة خبرهم للواقع، ولو بحسب اعتقادهم، كما في قوله : «كل ذلك لم يكن» لما قال له ذو اليدين : أقصرتِ الصلاةُ أم نسيت يا رسول الله? حين سلَّم من ركعتين (١).
فإن قيل : «قد مرَّ على جماعةٍ يُؤَبِّرون النخل، وقال لهم : لو تركتموها لصلحت، فتركوها فشاصت» (٢)، أجيب أن هذا من قبيل الإنشاء; لأنَّ المعنى : كان في رجائي ذلك، والإنشاء لا يتصف بصدق ولا كذب، وعدم وقوع المُترجى لا يعد نقصاً، ودليل وجوب صدقهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام أنهم لو لم يصدقوا للزم الكذب في خبره تعالى لتصديقه تعالى لهم بالمعجزة النازلة منزلة قوله تعالى : (صدق عبدي في كل ما يبلغ عني)، وتصديق الكاذب كذب; وهو محال في حقه تعالى، فملزومه وهو عدم صدقهم محال ، وإذا استحال عدم صدقهم وجب صدقهم وهو المطلوب، لكن هذا الدليل إنما يدلُّ على صدقهم في دعوى الرسالة وفي الأحكام الشرعية; لأن ذلك هو الذي بلغوه عن الله تعالى ولا يدل على صدقهم في غير ذلك كـ (قام زيد، وقعد عمرو)، ولكن يدل عليه دليل الأمانة; لأنه داخل فيها، ولو التفت لعموم الأمانة ما بعدها، وعُلم من ذلك أنَّ أقسام الصدق ثلاثة، والمقصود هنا جميع الأولان، وأما الثالث فهو داخل في الأمانة كما علمت.
His saying: 'and their truthfulness' is conjoined to trustworthiness; i.e., obligatory in their right is their truthfulness, which is the correspondence of their report with reality, even if according to their belief, as in his saying: 'All that did not happen' when Dhu al-Yadayn said to him: 'Was the prayer shortened or did you forget, O Messenger of Allah?' when he had given the salam after two rak'ahs (1). If it is said: 'He passed by a group pollinating palm trees and said to them: If you leave them, they will be fine, so they left them and they became spoiled' (2), the answer is that this is of the category of construction (insha'), because the meaning is: that was my hope; and construction is not described as true or false, and the non-occurrence of what is hoped for does not constitute a deficiency. And the proof for the obligation of their truthfulness (peace and blessings be upon them) is that if they were not truthful, then falsehood would be necessary in the news of Allah the Exalted, due to His confirmation of them through the miracle, which is in the position of His saying: 'My servant is truthful in all he conveys from Me.' And confirming a liar is a lie; that is impossible with respect to Allah the Exalted. So its entailment—which is their lack of truthfulness—is impossible. And if their lack of truthfulness is impossible, then their truthfulness is necessary, and that is what is sought. However, this proof only indicates their truthfulness in the claim of prophethood and in legal rulings, because that is what they conveyed from Allah the Exalted, and it does not indicate their truthfulness in other matters like 'Zaid stood' or 'Amr sat.' But that is indicated by the proof of trustworthiness, because it is included in it. And if you consider the generality of trustworthiness, it covers that. And it is known from this that the categories of truthfulness are three, and what is intended here are the first two, while the third is included in trustworthiness as you have learned.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines truthfulness for prophets, addresses an objection, and proves its necessity. It begins by explaining that truthfulness (الصدق) is the correspondence of their report with reality, even if only according to their belief. An example is given from a hadith where the Prophet said 'All that did not happen' after a forgetful salam. Then an objection is raised: the Prophet once told palm tree growers to leave them, then they became spoiled, which might seem like a false prediction. The answer is that this statement was a construction (إنشاء), i.e., an expression of hope, not a report, so it is not evaluated as true or false. The proof for truthfulness is that if prophets were not truthful, then Allah's confirmation of them through miracles would be confirming a liar, which is impossible, so truthfulness is necessary. However, this proof only covers their truthfulness in matters of prophethood and religious rulings; other matters are covered by the proof of trustworthiness. The passage ends by noting that there are three categories of truthfulness, and the intent here includes the first two, while the third falls under trustworthiness.

Key Terms
الصدق
الإنشاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Prophet made a false prediction about the palm trees, thus violating truthfulness.
Correction: The statement was an expression of hope, not a report of future fact, so it does not involve truth or falsehood.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection about the Prophet's statement to the palm tree growers?
A) The Prophet made a mistake.
B) The statement was a construction (إنشاء), not a report.
C) The growers misunderstood.
D) The hadith is weak.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic truthfulness is defined as correspondence with reality, and statements of hope are not reports, so they neither confirm nor violate truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about something you are not certain of, do you present it as a fact or as a hope? How does the distinction between khabar and insha' challenge the way you communicate with others about future events?
?
The Prophet’s truthfulness was upheld even in a statement based on his belief. Reflect on a time you stated something as true that you later discovered was false. Did you intend it as a report or as an expression of hope? What will you change in how you speak about uncertain matters?
?
If your words were judged by the standard of correspondence with reality, how many of your daily statements would pass as truthful reports? What would it cost you to only say what you know to be true, and to frame everything else as hope or opinion?

The Proof for Prophetic Truthfulness and Its Scope
Presents the proof for truthfulness and clarifies its scope and categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof from divine confirmation for prophetic truthfulness and its limitation to religious matters.

Core Idea
Allah's confirmation via miracles proves prophets truthful in revelation; trustworthiness covers other statements.

After establishing the definition and addressing an objection, the author now provides the rational proof that prophetic truthfulness is obligatory. The proof hinges on the fact that Allah confirms prophets by miracles, which would be impossible if they were liars. However, this proof only applies to their messages about prophethood and religious rulings; other statements are covered by the proof of trustworthiness.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof for truthfulness
Read the logical argument: if prophets were not truthful, then Allah's confirmation through miracles would be confirming a liar, which is impossible, so truthfulness is necessary.
Notice the phrase 'نازلة منزلة قوله تعالى: صدق عبدي'.
2
Understand the scope of the proof
The proof only guarantees truthfulness in matters of prophethood and religious rulings (what they convey from Allah). It does not cover statements about everyday matters.
The author says: 'لكن هذا الدليل إنما يدل على صدقهم في دعوى الرسالة وفي الأحكام الشرعية'.
3
Learn about the three categories of truthfulness
The passage concludes by stating that there are three categories of truthfulness. The first two are covered by this proof, and the third is covered by trustworthiness.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that Allah would never confirm a liar, so He confirmed the prophets with miracles. Have you fully accepted the teachings of the prophets, or do you sometimes doubt or reject parts of what they conveyed because they don't align with your desires? What would it mean for your faith if you are actually rejecting the truth that Allah Himself confirmed? Commit today to studying one prophetic teaching you have avoided.
?
If Allah considered confirming a liar as repugnant, how do you feel about your own role in confirming falsehood? When you remain silent about a lie, spread gossip, or approve of dishonesty for convenience, you are effectively confirming a liar. How will you change your speech and actions today to be a person who upholds truth even when it is difficult?
